  * George Saintsbury; A Short history of English Literature 319죇, Three
quartrains, not connected by any necessary or usual community of rhyme are tipped
with a couplet: and generally speaking, though not invariably, the opposition or balance
of octave and sestet which the Petrarchian form naturally invites is replaced here by a
steady building up of thought the douzain, and then either a climax or a quick
anti-strophe in the final couplet.

  *Otis and Needleman톩 ?픹 164죇 Arrangement of the sonnets. the general opinion
seems to be that Nos. 1-C24 are substantially the sequence in which the sonnets were
originally written. Among the groups apparently in their proper order are Nos. 1-17,
33-10L2, L13-L15, L28-L34. Not only has it been shown that the Quarto arrangement
is not Shakespeare's but also that the arrangement is neither according to chronology or
subject.)

  *Legouisr & Cazamian: A History Of English Literature 428죇. The name of Juliet at
once calls up the Capulets'ball-room, or the moonlit balcony, or the tomb in which she
lay before she died. The trees of the Forest of Arden droop and rustle about Rosalind.
The storm blows upon the dishevelled Lear on the deserted heath. Hamlet Ivaits fever
ishly for tile ghost on the platform at Elsinore or cracks grim jokes in the Carchyard.
Nowhere is there more of the picturesque or of the poetry of nature than in
these Plays, Performed with a few Properties to symbolize rather than toindicate the
places in which their action passed.

  * Moody톩 ?픹, 128죇. The tavern was for the 17th century what the coffee-house
was for the 18th, a rallying-place for literary men: and Jonson is almost as typical a
tavern figure as Falstaff. His "mountain belly and his rocky face", his bluff
domineering personality, ruled by royal right the bohemian circle which gathered
at "The Mermaid" or The devil and St. Dunstan," where the young fellows of the
"tribe of Ben heard words: so nimble and so full of subtle flame / As if that every
one from whence they came / Had meant to put his whole wit in a jest, / And had
resolved to live a fool the rest / of his dull life.

  *Saintsbury톩 ?픹, 411죇.
The very term metaphysical has been quarralled with, and not quite unjustly, but
there is also some justice in itself... But it is not inappropriatly used for the habit,
common to this school of poets of always seeking to express something after, something
behind the simple, obvious first sense and suggestion of a subject. Johnson
has indeed not made so much of Cis term as he might: for he himserf only attributes
to his metaphysicals as their differentia, learning, with a kind of misplaced wit,
and the desire to say something that had never been said before.

  * Lord, who cretest man is wealth and stone
  Though foolishly he lost the same
  Decaying more and more
  Till the became
  Most Poor
  With thee
  0 let me rise
  As larks, harmoniously,
  And sing this day thy victories:
  That shall the fall further the flight in me.
  My tender age is sorrow did begin:
  And still with sickness and shame
  Thou didst so punish sin
  That I became
  Most thin
  With thee
  Let me combine
  And feel this day' thy victory
  For, if 1 limp any wing on thine,
  Affliction shall advance the flight in me.

?픹 569죇. Nothing so marvellous had been written in the mood of this ode. The
stanza of eight lines of different measure closed by an alexandrine, has both swing and
majesty. The poem has, above all that which no one else has possessed in the bane
degree as Milton- absolute perfection in the choice of words and sonorities
The imgagery is restrained and has an unequalled Bower of suggestion. Milton was
well versed in all these pagan religions which he condemns and was charmed by the
strange forms they sheltered.

  Lycidas (p.170)
  래덒(1637)톏 ?램톩 쟞톛 ?톩 늝뾵톓 촻봶丹 빪몬 쟧?밻 덒톋씉

?래봞.*
  * Alas! what boots it with uncessant care
  To tend the homely, slighted shepherd's trade,
  And strictly medltate the thankless use?
  Were it not better done, as others use!,
  To sport with Amaryllis in the shade
  Or with the tangles of Nature's rain?

  * Bangh톩 ?픹, 679죇 The elegy, like the sonnet, is the outcry of
one spur-galled by Fame, which he can all by no better title than "the
last infrmity of noble mind," and can hope for nowhere but in heaven. It
is not easy thing to make oneself the last of giant race. Nothing
would satify Milton but to be a Sponsor, and nothing that his age
created seemed to be above the stature of a Marvell. This is the
heart-breaking truth of Lycidas.

refuge from life. Milton made no distinction between life and lterature His
writings, both in prose and verse, reject his heroic life. His thinking is of
the same fine quality whether he is serving as Latin Secretary or penning an
epic poem. He is not merely a lofty poet but anoble character.

  All is best, though we oft doubt
  What th' unsearchable dispose
  Of highest wisdom brings about,
  And ever best found in the close.
  0ft he seems to hide his face,
  But unexpectedly returns,
  And to his faithful champion hath in place
  Bore witness gloriously,

  * To My Dear friend Mr. Congreve, on his Comedy called the Double Dealer.
  Well then the promised hour is come at last.
  But both to Congreve justly small submit
  One matched in judgment, both o'ermatched in wit.
  In him all beauties of the age we see
  Etherege his courtship, southerners purity,
  The satire, wit and strength of manly Wycherley....
